       <tr>
        <td style="padding:0.2em 0 0.4em">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saint_Pierre_tentant_de_marcher_sur_les_eaux_by_Fran%C3%A7ois_Boucher.jpg">
          <img alt="Saint Pierre tentant de marcher sur les eaux by François Boucher.jpg" data-file-height="619" data-file-width="439" height="310" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Saint_Pierre_tentant_de_marcher_sur_les_eaux_by_Fran%C3%A7ois_Boucher.jpg/220px-Saint_Pierre_tentant_de_marcher_sur_les_eaux_by_Fran%C3%A7ois_Boucher.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Saint_Pierre_tentant_de_marcher_sur_les_eaux_by_Fran%C3%A7ois_Boucher.jpg/330px-Saint_Pierre_tentant_de_marcher_sur_les_eaux_by_Fran%C3%A7ois_Boucher.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/31/Saint_Pierre_tentant_de_marcher_sur_les_eaux_by_Fran%C3%A7ois_Boucher.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
         </a>
         <div style="padding-top:0.2em;line-height:1.2em">
          One of the many supernatural acts attributed to
          <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">
           Jesus
          </a>
          ,
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesus_walks_on_water" title="Jesus walks on water">
           walking on water
          </a>
          . Art by
          <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Boucher" title="François Boucher">
           François Boucher
          </a>
          Cathédrale Saint-Louis (1766)
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Versailles_(city)" title="Versailles (city)">
           Versailles
          </a>
         </div>
        </td>

           <a href="/wiki/Scientific_evidence" title="Scientific evidence">
            Scientific evidence
           </a>
          </li>

       <tr>
        <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%">
         <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
          <ul>
           <li class="nv-view">
            <a href="/wiki/Template:Paranormal" title="Template:Paranormal">
             <abbr title="View this template">
              v
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-talk">
            <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Paranormal" title="Template talk:Paranormal">
             <abbr title="Discuss this template">
              t
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-edit">
            <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Paranormal&amp;action=edit">
             <abbr title="Edit this template">
              e
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The
       <b>
        supernatural
       </b>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Latin" title="Medieval Latin">
        Medieval Latin
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        supernātūrālis
       </i>
       :
       <i>
        supra
       </i>
       "above" +
       <i>
        naturalis
       </i>
       "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural" title="Natural">
        natural
       </a>
       ", first used: 1520–1530 AD)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       are those things, or things that have been claimed to exist, which cannot be explained by the laws of nature, including things characteristic of or relating to
       <a href="/wiki/Ghost" title="Ghost">
        ghosts
       </a>
       , gods, or other
       <a href="/wiki/Non-physical_entity" title="Non-physical entity">
        types of spirits and other non-material beings
       </a>
       , or to things beyond nature.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Things such as
       <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">
        lightning
       </a>
       which were once thought to be supernatural have been shown to be entirely naturalistic, and some people claim that there is nothing supernatural. Such people maintain a skeptical attitude and belief. At least one prize for proving the existence of any supernatural thing at all remains unclaimed.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Supernaturalism">
        Supernaturalism
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernatural&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Supernaturalism">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(philosophy)" title="Naturalism (philosophy)">
        Naturalism (philosophy)
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <i>
        Supernaturalism
       </i>
       , as opposed to
       <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(philosophy)" title="Naturalism (philosophy)">
        naturalism
       </a>
       , is a belief in the supernatural in interpreting the world or attempting to control it. It can vary from those who believe that supernatural powers or entities are constantly or continuously intervening in the natural world to those who, like
       <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">
        Deists
       </a>
       , believe that only the origins of the natural world and its laws should be sought in the supernatural.
      </p>
      <p>
       The supernatural is a feature of the philosophical traditions of
       <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">
        Neoplatonism
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">
        Scholasticism
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In contrast, the philosophy of
       <a href="/wiki/Metaphysical_naturalism" title="Metaphysical naturalism">
        Metaphysical naturalism
       </a>
       argues for the conclusion that there are no supernatural entities, objects, or powers.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">
        Religion
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernatural&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Religion">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">
        Religion
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Magic_and_religion" title="Magic and religion">
        Magic and religion
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       All
       <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">
        religions
       </a>
       include elements of belief in the supernatural; from belief in the beyond natural abilities of
       <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">
        deities
       </a>
       to beliefs in
       <a href="/wiki/Spirit" title="Spirit">
        spirits
       </a>
       and other miracles such as for example
       <a href="/wiki/Saint#General_characteristics" title="Saint">
        miraculous works by recognized Saints
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Assumption_of_Mary" title="Assumption of Mary">
        Assumption of Mary
       </a>
       , etc. All, or virtually all religions include
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophesy" title="Prophesy">
        prophesies
       </a>
       supposedly communicated to
       <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">
        prophets
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The supernatural is also often featuring prominently in the study of the
       <a href="/wiki/Paranormal" title="Paranormal">
        paranormal
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occultism" title="Occultism">
        occultism
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Deity">
        Deity
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernatural&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Deity">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">
        Deity
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       A
       <i>
        deity
       </i>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'d' in 'dye'">
            d
           </span>
           <span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">
            iː
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">
            i
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
        <small class="nowrap">
         (
         <span class="unicode haudio">
          <span class="fn">
           <span style="white-space:nowrap">
            <a href="/wiki/File:En-uk-deity1.ogg" title="About this sound">
             <img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
            </a>
           </span>
           <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/22/En-uk-deity1.ogg" title="En-uk-deity1.ogg">
            listen
           </a>
          </span>
         </span>
         )
        </small>
       </span>
       or
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'d' in 'dye'">
            d
           </span>
           <span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">
            eɪ
           </span>
           <span title="/./: syllable break">
            .
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">
            i
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
        <small class="nowrap">
         (
         <span class="unicode haudio">
          <span class="fn">
           <span style="white-space:nowrap">
            <a href="/wiki/File:En-uk-deity2.ogg" title="About this sound">
             <img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
            </a>
           </span>
           <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3a/En-uk-deity2.ogg" title="En-uk-deity2.ogg">
            listen
           </a>
          </span>
         </span>
         )
        </small>
       </span>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       is a supernatural being considered
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divine" title="Divine">
        divine
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Sacred" title="Sacred">
        sacred
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OBrien_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-OBrien-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <i>
        Oxford Dictionary of English
       </i>
       defines deity as "a god or goddess (in a polytheistic religion)", or anything revered as divine.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stevenson_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Stevenson-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/C._Scott_Littleton" title="C. Scott Littleton">
        C. Scott Littleton
       </a>
       defines a deity as "a being with powers greater than those of ordinary humans, but who interacts with humans, positively or negatively, in ways that carry humans to new
       <a href="/wiki/Level_of_consciousness_(Esotericism)" title="Level of consciousness (Esotericism)">
        levels of consciousness,
       </a>
       beyond the grounded preoccupations of ordinary life."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A male deity is a
       <b>
        <a href="/wiki/God_(male_deity)" title="God (male deity)">
         god
        </a>
       </b>
       , while a female deity is a
       <b>
        <a href="/wiki/Goddess" title="Goddess">
         goddess
        </a>
       </b>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Religions can be categorized by how many deities they worship.
       <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">
        Monotheistic
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">
        religions
       </a>
       accept only one deity (predominantly referred to as God),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">
        polytheistic
       </a>
       religions accept multiple deities.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Henotheism" title="Henotheism">
        Henotheistic
       </a>
       religions accept one
       <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Being" title="Supreme Being">
        supreme deity
       </a>
       without denying other deities, considering them as equivalent aspects of the same divine principle;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Nontheistic_religion" title="Nontheistic religion">
        nontheistic religions
       </a>
       deny any supreme eternal
       <a href="/wiki/Creator_deity" title="Creator deity">
        creator deity
       </a>
       but accept a
       <a href="/wiki/Pantheon_(religion)" title="Pantheon (religion)">
        pantheon
       </a>
       of deities which live, die, and are reborn just like any other being.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keown_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Keown-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         35-37
        </span>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bullivant_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bullivant-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         357-358
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Various cultures have conceptualized a deity differently than a
       <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">
        monotheistic God
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hood_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hood-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trigger_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Trigger-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A deity need not be
       <a href="/wiki/Omnipotence" title="Omnipotence">
        omnipotent
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Omnipresence" title="Omnipresence">
        omnipresent
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Omniscience" title="Omniscience">
        omniscient
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Omnibenevolence" title="Omnibenevolence">
        omnibenevolent
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Immortality" title="Immortality">
        eternal
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hood_17-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hood-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trigger_18-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Trigger-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murdoch_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Murdoch-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The monotheistic God,however, does have these
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attributes_of_God" title="Attributes of God">
        attributes
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taliaferro_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Taliaferro-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilkerson_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Wilkerson-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Monotheistic religions typically refer to God in masculine terms,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OBrien2_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-OBrien2-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         96
        </span>
       </sup>
       while other religions refer to their deities in a variety of ways – masculine, feminine, androgynous and gender neutral.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Historically, many ancient cultures – such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian" title="Ancient Egyptian">
        Ancient Egyptian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">
        Ancient Greek
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">
        Ancient Roman
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Norsemen" title="Norsemen">
        Nordic
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Asia" title="Culture of Asia">
        Asian culture
       </a>
       – personified
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_natural_phenomena" title="List of natural phenomena">
        natural phenomena
       </a>
       , variously as either their conscious causes or simply their effects, respectively.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Malandra_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Malandra-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fløistad_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fløistad-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some
       <a href="/wiki/Avesta" title="Avesta">
        Avestan
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">
        Vedic
       </a>
       deities were viewed as ethical concepts.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Malandra_28-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Malandra-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fløistad_29-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fløistad-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Indian_religions" title="Indian religions">
        Indian religions
       </a>
       , deities have been envisioned as manifesting within the temple of every living being's body, as sensory organs and mind.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Deities have also been envisioned as a form of existence (
       <a href="/wiki/Sa%E1%B9%83s%C4%81ra" title="Saṃsāra">
        Saṃsāra
       </a>
       ) after
       <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">
        rebirth
       </a>
       , for human beings who gain merit through an ethical life, where they become
       <a href="/wiki/Tutelary_deity" title="Tutelary deity">
        guardian deities
       </a>
       and live blissfully in
       <a href="/wiki/Heaven" title="Heaven">
        heaven
       </a>
       , but are also subject to death when their merit runs out.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keown_15-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Keown-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         35-38
        </span>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bullivant_16-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bullivant-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         356-359
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Angel">
        Angel
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernatural&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Angel">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Angel" title="Angel">
        Angel
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Guido_Reni_031.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2358" data-file-width="1576" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Guido_Reni_031.jpg/200px-Guido_Reni_031.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Guido_Reni_031.jpg/300px-Guido_Reni_031.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Guido_Reni_031.jpg/400px-Guido_Reni_031.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Guido_Reni_031.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Michael_(archangel)" title="Michael (archangel)">
          Archangel Michael
         </a>
         wears a late Roman military cloak and cuirass in this 17th-century depiction by
         <a href="/wiki/Guido_Reni" title="Guido Reni">
          Guido Reni
         </a>

      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bernhard_Plockhorst_-_Schutzengel.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="917" data-file-width="658" height="307" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Bernhard_Plockhorst_-_Schutzengel.jpg/220px-Bernhard_Plockhorst_-_Schutzengel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Bernhard_Plockhorst_-_Schutzengel.jpg/330px-Bernhard_Plockhorst_-_Schutzengel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Bernhard_Plockhorst_-_Schutzengel.jpg/440px-Bernhard_Plockhorst_-_Schutzengel.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bernhard_Plockhorst_-_Schutzengel.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          Schutzengel
         </i>
         (English: "Guardian Angel") by
         <a href="/wiki/Bernhard_Plockhorst" title="Bernhard Plockhorst">
          Bernhard Plockhorst
         </a>
         depicts a
         <a href="/wiki/Guardian_angel" title="Guardian angel">
          guardian angel
         </a>
         watching over two children.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stift_Seitenstetten_Marmorsaal_Deckenfresko_01.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3936" data-file-width="5810" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Stift_Seitenstetten_Marmorsaal_Deckenfresko_01.JPG/220px-Stift_Seitenstetten_Marmorsaal_Deckenfresko_01.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Stift_Seitenstetten_Marmorsaal_Deckenfresko_01.JPG/330px-Stift_Seitenstetten_Marmorsaal_Deckenfresko_01.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Stift_Seitenstetten_Marmorsaal_Deckenfresko_01.JPG/440px-Stift_Seitenstetten_Marmorsaal_Deckenfresko_01.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stift_Seitenstetten_Marmorsaal_Deckenfresko_01.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          The Harmony between Religion and Science,
         </i>
         a ceiling
         <a href="/wiki/Frescobaldi" title="Frescobaldi">
          fresco
         </a>
         of the Marble Hall at Seitenstetten Abbey (Lower Austria) by
         <a href="/wiki/Paul_Troger" title="Paul Troger">
          Paul Troger
         </a>
         , 1735
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:An_allegory_of_poetry.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1995" data-file-width="2000" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/An_allegory_of_poetry.jpg/220px-An_allegory_of_poetry.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/An_allegory_of_poetry.jpg/330px-An_allegory_of_poetry.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/An_allegory_of_poetry.jpg/440px-An_allegory_of_poetry.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:An_allegory_of_poetry.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          An allegory of poetry
         </i>
         by
         <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Boucher" title="François Boucher">
          François Boucher
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jacob_Wrestling_with_the_Angel.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="795" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Jacob_Wrestling_with_the_Angel.jpg/220px-Jacob_Wrestling_with_the_Angel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Jacob_Wrestling_with_the_Angel.jpg/330px-Jacob_Wrestling_with_the_Angel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Jacob_Wrestling_with_the_Angel.jpg/440px-Jacob_Wrestling_with_the_Angel.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jacob_Wrestling_with_the_Angel.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Jacob_wrestling_with_the_angel" title="Jacob wrestling with the angel">
           Jacob Wrestling with the Angel
          </a>
         </i>
         , by
         <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Dor%C3%A9" title="Gustave Doré">
          Gustave Doré
         </a>
         in 1855
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       An
       <i>
        angel
       </i>
       is generally a supernatural being found in various
       <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">
        religions
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">
        mythologies
       </a>
       . In
       <a href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religions" title="Abrahamic religions">
        Abrahamic religions
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">
        Zoroastrianism
       </a>
       , angels are often depicted as benevolent celestial beings who act as intermediaries between
       <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">
        God
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Heaven" title="Heaven">
        Heaven
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
        Earth
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReligFacts_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ReligFacts-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Other roles of angels include protecting and guiding human beings, and carrying out God's tasks.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Within Abrahamic religions, angels are often organized into hierarchies, although such rankings may vary between sects in each religion, and are given specific names or titles, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Gabriel" title="Gabriel">
        Gabriel
       </a>
       or "
       <a href="/wiki/Destroying_angel_(Bible)" title="Destroying angel (Bible)">
        Destroying angel
       </a>
       ". The term "angel" has also been expanded to various notions of spirits or figures found in other religious traditions. The theological study of angels is known as "angelology".
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Angels_in_art" title="Angels in art">
        In fine art
       </a>
       , angels are usually depicted as having the shape of human beings of extraordinary beauty;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:2-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       they are often identified using the
       <a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">
        symbols
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Bird_flight" title="Bird flight">
        bird wings
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Halo_(religious_iconography)" title="Halo (religious iconography)">
        halos
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">
        light
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Prophecy">
        Prophecy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernatural&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Prophecy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Prophecy" title="Prophecy">
        Prophecy
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Prophecy involves a process in which one or more messages are allegedly communicated by a god to a
       <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">
        prophet
       </a>
       . Such messages typically involve inspiration, interpretation, or
       <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">
        revelation
       </a>
       of divine will concerning the prophet's social world and events to come (compare
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_(theology)" title="Light (theology)">
        divine knowledge
       </a>
       ). Prophecy is not limited to any one culture. It is a common property to all known ancient societies around the world, some more than others. Many systems and rules about prophecy have been proposed over several millennia.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Revelation">
        Revelation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernatural&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Revelation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">
        Revelation
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">
        religion
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">
        theology
       </a>
       , revelation is the revealing or disclosing of some form of
       <a href="/wiki/Religious_views_on_truth" title="Religious views on truth">
        truth
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Knowledge#Religious_meaning_of_knowledge" title="Knowledge">
        knowledge
       </a>
       through communication with a
       <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">
        deity
       </a>
       or other supernatural entity or entities.
      </p>
      <p>
       Some religions have
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_texts" title="Religious texts">
        religious texts
       </a>
       which they view as divinely or supernaturally revealed or inspired. For instance,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_Jews" title="Orthodox Jews">
        Orthodox Jews
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">
        Christians
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">
        Muslims
       </a>
       believe that the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">
         Torah
        </a>
       </i>
       was received from
       <a href="/wiki/Yahweh" title="Yahweh">
        Yahweh
       </a>
       on
       <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Mount_Sinai" title="Biblical Mount Sinai">
        biblical Mount Sinai
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Most Christians believe that both the
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">
        Old Testament
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">
        New Testament
       </a>
       were
       <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inspiration" title="Biblical inspiration">
        inspired
       </a>
       by God. Muslims believe the
       <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">
        Quran
       </a>
       was revealed by God to
       <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">
        Muhammad
       </a>
       word by word through the angel
       <a href="/wiki/Gabriel" title="Gabriel">
        Gabriel
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Jibril
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lambert_43-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lambert-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_&amp;_Drew_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Williams_&amp;_Drew-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">
        Hinduism
       </a>
       , some
       <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">
        Vedas
       </a>
       are considered
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apaurusheyatva" title="Apaurusheyatva">
         <i lang="sa-Latn" title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration" xml:lang="sa-Latn">
          apauruṣeya
         </i>
        </a>
       </i>
       , "not human compositions", and are supposed to have been directly revealed, and thus are called
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Aruti" title="Śruti">
         śruti
        </a>
       </i>
       , "what is heard". The 15,000 handwritten pages produced by the mystic
       <a href="/wiki/Maria_Valtorta" title="Maria Valtorta">
        Maria Valtorta
       </a>
       were represented as direct dictations from
       <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">
        Jesus
       </a>
       , while she attributed
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_Azariah" title="The Book of Azariah">
         The Book of Azariah
        </a>
       </i>
       to her
       <a href="/wiki/Guardian_angel" title="Guardian angel">
        guardian angel
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Aleister_Crowley" title="Aleister Crowley">
        Aleister Crowley
       </a>
       stated that
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_the_Law" title="The Book of the Law">
         The Book of the Law
        </a>
       </i>
       had been revealed to him through a higher being that called itself
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Aiwass" title="Aiwass">
         Aiwass
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       A revelation communicated by a supernatural entity reported as being present during the event is called a
       <a href="/wiki/Vision_(spirituality)" title="Vision (spirituality)">
        vision
       </a>
       . Direct conversations between the recipient and the supernatural entity,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       or physical marks such as
       <a href="/wiki/Stigmata" title="Stigmata">
        stigmata
       </a>
       , have been reported. In rare cases, such as that of Saint
       <a href="/wiki/Juan_Diego" title="Juan Diego">
        Juan Diego
       </a>
       , physical artifacts accompany the revelation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">
        Roman Catholic
       </a>
       concept of
       <a href="/wiki/Interior_locution" title="Interior locution">
        interior locution
       </a>
       includes just an inner voice heard by the recipient.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the
       <a href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religions" title="Abrahamic religions">
        Abrahamic religions
       </a>
       , the term is used to refer to the process by which
       <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">
        God
       </a>
       reveals knowledge of himself, his
       <a href="/wiki/Will_of_God" title="Will of God">
        will
       </a>
       , and his
       <a href="/wiki/Divine_providence" title="Divine providence">
        divine providence
       </a>
       to the world of human beings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In secondary usage, revelation refers to the resulting human knowledge about God,
       <a href="/wiki/Prophecy" title="Prophecy">
        prophecy
       </a>
       , and other
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divine" title="Divine">
        divine
       </a>
       things. Revelation from a supernatural source plays a less important role in some other religious traditions such as
       <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">
        Buddhism
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">
        Confucianism
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">
        Taoism
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Christian_theology">
        Christian theology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernatural&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Christian theology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:San_Giuseppe_di_Copertino_18th_century_engraving.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="458" data-file-width="305" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/San_Giuseppe_di_Copertino_18th_century_engraving.jpg/220px-San_Giuseppe_di_Copertino_18th_century_engraving.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/94/San_Giuseppe_di_Copertino_18th_century_engraving.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:San_Giuseppe_di_Copertino_18th_century_engraving.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Patron_saint" title="Patron saint">
          patron saint
         </a>
         of air travelers, aviators, astronauts, people with a mental handicap, test takers, and poor students is
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Joseph_of_Cupertino" title="Saint Joseph of Cupertino">
          Saint Joseph of Cupertino
         </a>
         , who is said to have been gifted with
         <a href="/wiki/Saints_and_levitation" title="Saints and levitation">
          supernatural flight
         </a>
         .
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
          <a href="#cite_note-49">
           [49]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Supernatural_order" title="Supernatural order">
        Supernatural order
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Catholic_theology" title="Catholic theology">
        Catholic theology
       </a>
       , the supernatural order is, according to
       <a href="/wiki/New_Advent" title="New Advent">
        New Advent
       </a>
       , defined as "the ensemble of effects exceeding the powers of the created universe and gratuitously produced by God for the purpose of raising the rational creature above its native sphere to a God-like life and destiny."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NA_50-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NA-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <i>
        Modern Catholic Dictionary
       </i>
       defines it as "the sum total of heavenly destiny and all the divinely established means of reaching that destiny, which surpass the mere powers and capacities of human nature."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Process_theology">
        Process theology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernatural&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Process theology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Process_theology" title="Process theology">
        Process theology
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Process theology is a school of thought influenced by the metaphysical
       <a href="/wiki/Process_philosophy" title="Process philosophy">
        process philosophy
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead" title="Alfred North Whitehead">
        Alfred North Whitehead
       </a>
       (1861–1947) and further developed by
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_Hartshorne" title="Charles Hartshorne">
        Charles Hartshorne
       </a>
       (1897–2000).
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        It is not possible, in process metaphysics, to conceive divine activity as a “supernatural” intervention into the “natural” order of events. Process theists usually regard the distinction between the supernatural and the natural as a by-product of the doctrine of creation
        <i>
         ex nihilo
        </i>
        . In process thought, there is no such thing as a realm of the natural in contrast to that which is supernatural. On the other hand, if “the natural” is defined more neutrally as “what is in the nature of things,” then process metaphysics characterizes the natural as the creative activity of actual entities. In Whitehead's words, “It lies in the nature of things that the many enter into complex unity” (Whitehead 1978, 21). It is tempting to emphasize process theism's denial of the supernatural and thereby highlight that the processed God cannot do in comparison what the traditional God could do (that is, to bring something from nothing). In fairness, however, equal stress should be placed on process theism's denial of the natural (as traditionally conceived) so that one may highlight what the creatures cannot do, in traditional theism, in comparison to what they can do in process metaphysics (that is, to be part creators of the world with God).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sep-process-theism_52-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-sep-process-theism-52">
          [52]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
       <div class="templatequotecite">
        —
        <cite>
         Donald Viney, "Process Theism" in
         <i>
          The Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy
         </i>
        </cite>
       </div>
      </blockquote>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spirit">
        Spirit
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernatural&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Spirit">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Spirit" title="Spirit">
        Spirit
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">
        Spirituality
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Theodor_von_Holst_Bertalda_Assailed_Spirits.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="838" data-file-width="519" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Theodor_von_Holst_Bertalda_Assailed_Spirits.png/170px-Theodor_von_Holst_Bertalda_Assailed_Spirits.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Theodor_von_Holst_Bertalda_Assailed_Spirits.png/255px-Theodor_von_Holst_Bertalda_Assailed_Spirits.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Theodor_von_Holst_Bertalda_Assailed_Spirits.png/340px-Theodor_von_Holst_Bertalda_Assailed_Spirits.png 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Theodor_von_Holst_Bertalda_Assailed_Spirits.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Theodor_von_Holst" title="Theodor von Holst">
          Theodor von Holst
         </a>
         ,
         <i>
          Bertalda, Assailed by Spirits
         </i>
         , c. 1830
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       A
       <i>
        spirit
       </i>
       is a supernatural being, often but not exclusively a
       <a href="/wiki/Non-physical_entity" title="Non-physical entity">
        non-physical entity
       </a>
       ; such as a
       <a href="/wiki/Ghost" title="Ghost">
        ghost
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Fairy" title="Fairy">
        fairy
       </a>
       , or
       <a href="/wiki/Angel" title="Angel">
        angel
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polysemy_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-polysemy-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The concepts of a person's spirit and
       <a href="/wiki/Soul" title="Soul">
        soul
       </a>
       , often also overlap, as both are either
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mind-body_dualism" title="Mind-body dualism">
        contrasted with
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Idealism" title="Idealism">
        given ontological priority over
       </a>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/Human_body" title="Human body">
        body
       </a>
       and both are believed to survive bodily death in some religions,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and "spirit" can also have the sense of "
       <a href="/wiki/Ghost" title="Ghost">
        ghost
       </a>
       ", i.e. a manifestation of the spirit of a deceased person. In English
       <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">
        Bibles
       </a>
       , "the Spirit" (with a capital "S"), specifically denotes the
       <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">
        Holy Spirit
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Spirit is often used
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metaphysically" title="Metaphysically">
        metaphysically
       </a>
       to refer to the
       <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">
        consciousness
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Personality" title="Personality">
        personality
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Historically, it was also used to refer to a "subtle" as opposed to "gross" material substance, as in the famous last paragraph of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Isaac_Newton" title="Sir Isaac Newton">
        Sir Isaac Newton
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica">
         Principia Mathematica
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Demon">
        Demon
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernatural&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Demon">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PazuzuDemonAssyria1stMil_2.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1736" data-file-width="1102" height="394" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/PazuzuDemonAssyria1stMil_2.jpg/250px-PazuzuDemonAssyria1stMil_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/PazuzuDemonAssyria1stMil_2.jpg/375px-PazuzuDemonAssyria1stMil_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/PazuzuDemonAssyria1stMil_2.jpg/500px-PazuzuDemonAssyria1stMil_2.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PazuzuDemonAssyria1stMil_2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Bronze statuette of the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyro-Babylonian" title="Assyro-Babylonian">
          Assyro-Babylonian
         </a>
         demon king
         <a href="/wiki/Pazuzu" title="Pazuzu">
          Pazuzu
         </a>
         , circa 800 BC –- circa 700 BC,
         <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">
          Louvre
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       A
       <i>
        demon
       </i>
       (from
       <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">
        Koine Greek
       </a>
       <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
        δαιμόνιον
       </span>
       <i>
        daimónion
       </i>
       ) is a supernatural and often malevolent being prevalent in
       <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">
        religion
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occultism" title="Occultism">
        occultism
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">
        literature
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Fiction" title="Fiction">
        fiction
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">
        mythology
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">
        folklore
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_Eastern_religions" title="Ancient Near Eastern religions">
        Ancient Near Eastern religions
       </a>
       as well as in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abrahamic_traditions" title="Abrahamic traditions">
        Abrahamic traditions
       </a>
       , including ancient and medieval
       <a href="/wiki/Christian_demonology" title="Christian demonology">
        Christian demonology
       </a>
       , a demon is considered a harmful spiritual entity, below the heavenly planes
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which may cause
       <a href="/wiki/Demonic_possession" title="Demonic possession">
        demonic possession
       </a>
       , calling for an
       <a href="/wiki/Exorcism" title="Exorcism">
        exorcism
       </a>
       . In Western
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occultism" title="Occultism">
        occultism
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_magic" title="Renaissance magic">
        Renaissance magic
       </a>
       , which grew out of an amalgamation of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_magic" title="Greco-Roman magic">
        Greco-Roman magic
       </a>
       , Jewish
       <a href="/wiki/Aggadah" title="Aggadah">
        Aggadah
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Christian_demonology" title="Christian demonology">
        Christian demonology
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       a demon is believed to be a spiritual entity that may be
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conjuration" title="Conjuration">
        conjured
       </a>
       and controlled.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Magic">
        Magic
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">

         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Magic_(paranormal)" title="Magic (paranormal)">
        Magic (paranormal)
       </a>
      </div>
