       <tr>
        <td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;padding-top:0.15em;">
         <a href="/wiki/Social_anthropology" title="Social anthropology">
          Social
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Cultural_anthropology" title="Cultural anthropology">
          cultural anthropology
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
         <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
          <ul>
           <li class="nv-view">
            <a href="/wiki/Template:Anthropology_of_religion" title="Template:Anthropology of religion">
             <abbr title="View this template">
              v
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-talk">
            <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Anthropology_of_religion" title="Template talk:Anthropology of religion">
             <abbr title="Discuss this template">
              t
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-edit">
            <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Anthropology_of_religion&amp;action=edit">
             <abbr title="Edit this template">
              e
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <i>
        Magic
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        sorcery
       </i>
       is the use of
       <a href="/wiki/Ritual" title="Ritual">
        rituals
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">
        symbols
       </a>
       , actions,
       <a href="/wiki/Gesture" title="Gesture">
        gestures
       </a>
       , or
       <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">
        language
       </a>
       with the aim of utilizing supernatural forces.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tambiah_59-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tambiah-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         6–7
        </span>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanegraaff_60-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hanegraaff-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mauss_61-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Mauss-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         24
        </span>
       </sup>
       Belief in and practice of magic has been present since the earliest human cultures and continues to have an important spiritual, religious, and medicinal role in many cultures today. The term magic has a variety of meanings, and there is no widely agreed upon definition of what it is.
      </p>
      <p>
       Scholars of religion have defined magic in different ways. One approach, associated with the
       <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">
        anthropologists
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_Tylor" title="Edward Tylor">
        Edward Tylor
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_G._Frazer" title="James G. Frazer">
        James G. Frazer
       </a>
       , suggests that magic and
       <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">
        science
       </a>
       are opposites. An alternative approach, associated with the
       <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">
        sociologists
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Mauss" title="Marcel Mauss">
        Marcel Mauss
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emile_Durkheim" title="Emile Durkheim">
        Emile Durkheim
       </a>
       , argues that magic takes place in private, while
       <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">
        religion
       </a>
       is a communal and organised activity. Many scholars of religion have rejected the utility of the term
       <i>
        magic
       </i>
       and it has become increasingly unpopular within scholarship since the 1990s.
      </p>
      <p>
       The term
       <i>
        magic
       </i>
       comes from the
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">
        Old Persian
       </a>
       <i>
        magu
       </i>
       , a word that applied to a form of religious functionary about which little is known. During the late sixth and early fifth centuries BCE, this term was adopted into
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">
        Ancient Greek
       </a>
       , where it was used with negative connotations, to apply to religious rites that were regarded as fraudulent, unconventional, and dangerous. This meaning of the term was then adopted by
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       in the first century BCE. The concept was then incorporated into
       <a href="/wiki/Christian_theology" title="Christian theology">
        Christian theology
       </a>
       during the first century CE, where magic was associated with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demons" title="Demons">
        demons
       </a>
       and thus defined against religion. This concept was pervasive throughout the Middle Ages, although in the early modern period Italian
       <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">
        humanists
       </a>
       reinterpreted the term in a positive sense to establish the idea of
       <a href="/wiki/Natural_magic" title="Natural magic">
        natural magic
       </a>
       . Both negative and positive understandings of the term were retained in Western culture over the following centuries, with the former largely influencing early academic usages of the word.
      </p>
      <p>
       Throughout history, there have been examples of individuals who practiced magic and referred to themselves as magicians. This trend has proliferated in the modern period, with a growing number of magicians appearing within the
       <a href="/wiki/Western_esotericism" title="Western esotericism">
        esoteric
       </a>
       milieu.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources in the body of the article. (November 2017)">
           not verified in body
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       British esotericist
       <a href="/wiki/Aleister_Crowley" title="Aleister Crowley">
        Aleister Crowley
       </a>
       described magic as the art of effecting change in accordance with will.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Miracle">
        Miracle
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernatural&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Miracle">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Miracle" title="Miracle">
        Miracle
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       A
       <i>
        miracle
       </i>
       is an event not explicable by natural or scientific laws.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Such an event may be attributed to a supernatural being (a
       <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">
        deity
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Magic_(paranormal)" title="Magic (paranormal)">
        magic
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Thaumaturgy" title="Thaumaturgy">
        miracle worker
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">
        saint
       </a>
       or a religious leader.
      </p>
      <p>
       Informally, the word "miracle" is often used to characterise any beneficial event that is statistically unlikely but not contrary to the laws of nature, such as surviving a natural disaster, or simply a "wonderful" occurrence, regardless of likelihood, such as a birth. Other such miracles might be: survival of an illness diagnosed as terminal, escaping a life-threatening situation or 'beating the odds'. Some
       <a href="/wiki/Coincidence" title="Coincidence">
        coincidences
       </a>
       may be seen as miracles.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A true miracle would, by definition, be a non-natural phenomenon, leading many rational and scientific thinkers to dismiss them as physically impossible (that is, requiring violation of established laws of physics within their domain of validity) or impossible to confirm by their nature (because all possible physical mechanisms can never be ruled out). The former position is expressed for instance by
       <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">
        Thomas Jefferson
       </a>
       and the latter by
       <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">
        David Hume
       </a>
       .
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theologian" title="Theologian">
        Theologians
       </a>
       typically say that, with
       <a href="/wiki/Divine_providence" title="Divine providence">
        divine providence
       </a>
       , God regularly works through nature yet, as a creator, is free to work without, above, or against it as well. The possibility and probability of miracles are then equal to the possibility and probability of the
       <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">
        existence of God
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miracles_64-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Miracles-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Skepticism">
        Skepticism
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernatural&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Skepticism">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Skepticism" title="Skepticism">
        Skepticism
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Skepticism (
       <a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">
        American English
       </a>
       ) or scepticism (
       <a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">
        British English
       </a>
       ;
       <a href='/wiki/American_and_British_English_spelling_differences#Hard_and_soft_"c"' title="American and British English spelling differences">
        see spelling differences
       </a>
       ) is generally any questioning attitude or
       <a href="/wiki/Doubt" title="Doubt">
        doubt
       </a>
       towards one or more items of putative knowledge or belief.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is often directed at domains such as the supernatural, morality (
       <a href="/wiki/Moral_skepticism" title="Moral skepticism">
        moral skepticism
       </a>
       ), religion (skepticism about the existence of God), or knowledge (skepticism about the possibility of knowledge, or of certainty).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Formally, skepticism as a topic occurs in the context of philosophy, particularly
       <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">
        epistemology
       </a>
       , although it can be applied to any topic such as politics, religion, and pseudoscience.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Epistemology_and_metaphysics">
        Epistemology and metaphysics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernatural&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Epistemology and metaphysics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Anthropology_of_religion" title="Anthropology of religion">
        Anthropology of religion
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">
        metaphysical
       </a>
       considerations of the existence of the supernatural can be difficult to approach as an exercise in philosophy or theology because any dependencies on its antithesis, the
       <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(philosophy)" title="Naturalism (philosophy)">
        natural
       </a>
       , will ultimately have to be inverted or rejected.
      </p>
      <p>
       One complicating factor is that there is disagreement about the definition of "natural" and the limits of
       <a href="/wiki/Metaphysical_naturalism" title="Metaphysical naturalism">
        naturalism
       </a>
       . Concepts in the supernatural domain are closely related to concepts in
       <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">
        religious
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">
        spirituality
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occultism" title="Occultism">
        occultism
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Spiritualism" title="Spiritualism">
        spiritualism
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        For sometimes we use the word
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">
          nature
         </a>
        </i>
        for that
        <i>
         Author of nature
        </i>
        whom the
        <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">
         schoolmen
        </a>
        , harshly enough, call
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Natura_naturans" title="Natura naturans">
          natura naturans
         </a>
        </i>
        , as when it is said that
        <i>
         nature
        </i>
        hath made man partly corporeal and
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mind-body_dualism" title="Mind-body dualism">
         partly immaterial
        </a>
        . Sometimes we mean by the
        <i>
         nature
        </i>
        of a thing the
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">
          essence
         </a>
        </i>
        , or that which the schoolmen scruple not to call the
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Quiddity" title="Quiddity">
          quiddity
         </a>
        </i>
        of a thing, namely, the
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property (philosophy)">
          attribute
         </a>
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         attributes
        </i>
        on whose score it is what it is, whether the thing be
        <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">
         corporeal
        </a>
        or not, as when we attempt to define the
        <i>
         nature
        </i>
        of an
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Angle" title="Angle">
          angle
         </a>
        </i>
        , or of a
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Triangle" title="Triangle">
          triangle
         </a>
        </i>
        , or of a
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Fluid" title="Fluid">
          fluid
         </a>
        </i>
        body, as such. Sometimes we take
        <i>
         nature
        </i>
        for an internal principle of
        <a href="/wiki/Motion_(physics)" title="Motion (physics)">
         motion
        </a>
        , as when we say that a stone let fall in the
        <a href="/wiki/Air_(classical_element)" title="Air (classical element)">
         air
        </a>
        is by
        <i>
         nature
        </i>
        carried towards the centre of the
        <a href="/wiki/Earth_(classical_element)" title="Earth (classical element)">
         earth
        </a>
        , and, on the contrary, that
        <a href="/wiki/Fire_(classical_element)" title="Fire (classical element)">
         fire
        </a>
        or flame does
        <i>
         naturally
        </i>
        move upwards toward
        <a href="/wiki/Firmament" title="Firmament">
         heaven
        </a>
        . Sometimes we understand by
        <i>
         nature
        </i>
        the established course of things, as when we say that
        <i>
         nature
        </i>
        makes the
        <a href="/wiki/Night" title="Night">
         night
        </a>
        succeed the
        <a href="/wiki/Day" title="Day">
         day
        </a>
        ,
        <i>
         nature
        </i>
        hath made
        <a href="/wiki/Respiration_(physiology)" title="Respiration (physiology)">
         respiration
        </a>
        necessary to the
        <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">
         life
        </a>
        of men. Sometimes we take
        <i>
         nature
        </i>
        for an
        <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">
         aggregate of powers
        </a>
        belonging to a body, especially a living one, as when
        <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">
         physicians
        </a>
        say that
        <i>
         nature
        </i>
        is strong or weak or spent, or that in such or such
        <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">
         diseases
        </a>
        <i>
         nature
        </i>
        left to herself
        <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">
         will do the cure
        </a>
        . Sometimes we take nature for the
        <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">
         universe
        </a>
        , or system of the corporeal works of
        <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">
         God
        </a>
        , as when it is said of a
        <a href="/wiki/Phoenix_(mythology)" title="Phoenix (mythology)">
         phoenix
        </a>
        , or a
        <a href="/wiki/Chimera_(mythology)" title="Chimera (mythology)">
         chimera
        </a>
        , that there is no such thing in
        <i>
         nature
        </i>
        , i.e. in the world. And sometimes too, and that most commonly, we would express by
        <i>
         nature
        </i>
        a
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mother_nature" title="Mother nature">
         semi-deity
        </a>
        or other strange kind of being, such as this discourse examines the notion of.
        <br/>
        <br/>
        And besides these more absolute acceptions, if I may so call them, of the word
        <i>
         nature
        </i>
        , it has divers others (more relative), as
        <i>
         nature
        </i>
        is wont to be set or in
        <a href="/wiki/Opposite_(semantics)" title="Opposite (semantics)">
         opposition
        </a>
        or contradistinction to other things, as when we say of a stone when it falls downwards that it does it by a
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_elements" title="Classical elements">
          natural motion
         </a>
        </i>
        , but that if it be thrown upwards its motion that way is
        <i>
         violent
        </i>
        . So chemists distinguish
        <a href="/wiki/Vitriol" title="Vitriol">
         vitriol
        </a>
        into
        <i>
         natural
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         fictitious
        </i>
        , or made by art, i.e. by the intervention of human power or skill; so it is said that
        <a href="/wiki/Water_(classical_element)" title="Water (classical element)">
         water
        </a>
        , kept suspended in a sucking pump, is not in its
        <i>
         natural
        </i>
        place, as that is which is stagnant in the well. We say also that wicked men are still in the state of
        <i>
         nature
        </i>
        , but the regenerate in a state of
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Divine_grace" title="Divine grace">
          grace
         </a>
        </i>
        ; that cures wrought by
        <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">
         medicines
        </a>
        are natural operations; but the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miraculous" title="Miraculous">
         miraculous
        </a>
        ones wrought by
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christ" title="Christ">
         Christ
        </a>
        and his
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apostle_(Christian)" title="Apostle (Christian)">
         apostles
        </a>
        were
        <i>
         supernatural
        </i>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
         <a href="#cite_note-68">
          [68]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
       <div class="templatequotecite">
        —
        <cite>
         <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">
          Robert Boyle
         </a>
         , A Free Enquiry into the Vulgarly Received Notion of Nature
        </cite>
       </div>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       The term "supernatural" is often used interchangeably with
       <a href="/wiki/Paranormal" title="Paranormal">
        paranormal
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Preternatural" title="Preternatural">
        preternatural
       </a>
       — the latter typically limited to an adjective for describing abilities which appear to exceed what is possible within the boundaries of the laws of physics.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">
        Epistemologically
       </a>
       , the relationship between the supernatural and the natural is indistinct in terms of natural phenomena that,
       <i>
        ex hypothesi,
       </i>
       violate the laws of nature, in so far as such laws are
       <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_realism" title="Philosophical realism">
        realistically accountable
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        Parapsychologists use the term psi to refer to an assumed unitary force underlying the phenomena they study. Psi is defined in the
        <i>
         Journal of Parapsychology
        </i>
        as “personal factors or processes in nature which transcend accepted laws” (1948: 311) and “which are
        <a href="/wiki/Non-physical_entity" title="Non-physical entity">
         non-physical
        </a>
        in nature” (1962:310), and it is used to cover both extrasensory perception (ESP), an “awareness of or response to an external event or influence not apprehended by sensory means” (1962:309) or inferred from sensory knowledge, and psychokinesis (PK), “the direct influence exerted on a physical system by a subject without any known intermediate energy or instrumentation” (1945:305).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winkelman1982_70-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Winkelman1982-70">
          [70]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
       <div class="templatequotecite">
        —
        <cite>
         Michael Winkelman, Current Anthropology
        </cite>
       </div>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       Many supporters of supernatural explanations believe that past, present, and future complexities and mysteries of the
       <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">
        universe
       </a>
       cannot be explained solely by naturalistic means and argue that it is reasonable to assume that a non-natural entity or entities resolve the unexplained.
      </p>
      <p>
       Views on the "supernatural" vary, for example it may be seen as:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <b>
         indistinct from nature
        </b>
        . From this perspective, some events occur according to the
        <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">
         laws of nature
        </a>
        , and others occur according to a separate set of principles external to known nature. For example, in Scholasticism, it was believed that God was capable of performing any miracle so long as it didn't lead to a logical
        <a href="/wiki/Contradiction" title="Contradiction">
         contradiction
        </a>
        . Some religions posit immanent deities, however, and do not have a tradition analogous to the supernatural; some believe that everything anyone experiences occurs by the will (
        <a href="/wiki/Occasionalism" title="Occasionalism">
         occasionalism
        </a>
        ), in the mind (
        <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">
         neoplatonism
        </a>
        ), or as a part (
        <a href="/wiki/Nondualism" title="Nondualism">
         nondualism
        </a>
        ) of a more fundamental divine reality (
        <a href="/wiki/Platonism" title="Platonism">
         platonism
        </a>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         incorrect human attribution
        </b>
        . In this view all events have natural and only natural causes. They believe that human beings ascribe supernatural attributes to purely natural events, such as
        <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">
         lightning
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Rainbow" title="Rainbow">
         rainbows
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Flood" title="Flood">
         floods
        </a>
        , and the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Origin_of_life" title="Origin of life">
         origin of life
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhong1976_71-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Zhong1976-71">
          [71]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis2004_72-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Ellis2004-72">
          [72]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>

         <span class="reference-text">

        <li id="cite_note-4">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-4">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          "The eventual development of a clear concept of the supernatural in Christian theology was promoted both by dialogues with heretics and by the influence of Neoplatonic philosophy."
          <a class="external text" href="http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1525/eth.1977.5.1.02a00040/pdf" rel="nofollow">
           Benson Saler:
           <i>
            Supernatural as a Western Category.
           </i>
          </a>
          Ethos 5 (1977): 44
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-5">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-5">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          "Saint Thomas's important contribution to the emergence of a technical theology of the supernatural represents a special development of the concept of surpassing effects. Saint Thomas and others of the Scholastics have left us as one of their legacies a dichotomy between the natural and the supernatural that is theologically rooted in the distinction between the Order of Nature and the Order of Grace."
          <a class="external text" href="http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1525/eth.1977.5.1.02a00040/pdf" rel="nofollow">
           Benson Saler:
           <i>
            Supernatural as a Western Category.
           </i>
          </a>
          Ethos 5 (1977): 47–48
         </span>
        </li>

           <q>
            The Christian tradition is, in imitation of Judaism, a monotheistic religion. This implies that believers accept the existence of only one God. Other deities either do not exist, are seen as the product of human imagination or are dismissed as remanents of a persistent paganism
           </q>

         <span class="reference-text">

           Trigger, Bruce G. (2003).
           <i>

           </i>

           <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">

           .
           <q>
            [Historically...] people perceived far fewer differences between themselves and the gods than the adherents of modern monotheistic religions. Deities were not thought to be omniscient or omnipotent and were rarely believed to be changeless or eternal
           </q>
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Understanding+Early+Civilizations%3A+A+Comparative+Study&amp;rft.place=Cambridge&amp;rft.pages=441-442&amp;rft.edition=1st&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2003&amp;rft.isbn=9780521822459&amp;rft.aulast=Trigger&amp;rft.aufirst=Bruce+G.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASupernatural">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-Murdoch-19">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-Murdoch_19-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          John Murdoch,
          <i>
           <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=IHQAAAAAMAAJ" rel="nofollow">
            English Translations of Select Tracts, Published in India - Religious Texts
           </a>
          </i>
          at
          <a href="/wiki/Google_Books" title="Google Books">
           Google Books
          </a>
          , pages 141-142;
          <b>
           Quote:
          </b>
          "We [monotheists] find by reason and revelation that God is omniscient, omnipotent, most holy, etc, but the Hindu deities possess none of those attributes. It is mentioned in their
          <a href="/wiki/Shastra" title="Shastra">
           Shastras
          </a>
          that their deities were all vanquished by the Asurs, while they fought in the heavens, and for fear of whom they left their abodes. This plainly shows that they are not omnipotent."
         </span>
        </li>

          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Origins+of+the+Social+Mind%3A+Evolutionary+Psychology+and+Child+Development&amp;rft.pages=413&amp;rft.pub=Guilford+Publications&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft_id=info%3Alccn%2F2004022693&amp;rft.isbn=9781593851033&amp;rft.aulast=Ellis&amp;rft.aufirst=B.J.&amp;rft.au=Bjorklund%2C+D.F.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D-UjiZwYGdFoC%26pg%3DPA413&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASupernatural">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
       </ol>
